Food is *segregated* — sandwich, fruit, snack, in that order, never overlapping. She does not eat while reading. She does not eat while standing. If a customer enters during this block she will close the bag, attend to them, and resume from the precise bite she stopped on.

**13:45–18:00.** Trading and bookkeeping. She does not take an afternoon break. Bookkeeping is done in fragments throughout the afternoon and consolidated into a clean ledger entry at end of day. The consolidation ritual takes approximately twenty-five minutes and is non-negotiable; she has been observed asking Buffy to please patrol-strategize in the front room because she is "doing the books."

**18:00–18:30.** Closing ritual. Mirror image of the opening, in reverse order. Float subtracted, day's take recorded, deposit bag prepared (she runs the deposit to the night drop herself, on foot, three nights a week; the other two it sits in the safe; weekend pattern below).

**18:30–22:30.** Variable. This is the *only* substantial unstructured block in her week, and it tracks her attachments. With Xander present: domestic, food-oriented, sex on a predictable two-to-three night cycle (see §4). With Xander absent: Scooby business at the Magic Box, training-room observation, magic-supply prep for Willow, or — if no Scooby task is available — return to apartment, television (game shows preferred, news avoided, weather watched obsessively).

**22:30–23:15.** Pre-sleep ritual. She brushes her teeth for the full duration of a song she hums under her breath (the song is the same one every night and is not identified in the canonical record; an analyst noting hummed melody over weeks would catalog it as a single fixed tune). She checks the door lock twice. She checks the windows. She places her shoes parallel and pointed toward the door.

**23:15–05:50.** Sleeps lightly. Wakes at small noises. Re-sleeps with difficulty.

---

## 2. The Week

Across a week, the day-structure above stays largely fixed Tuesday through Saturday. Monday and Sunday are the perturbations.

**Sunday** is closed-shop day; she uses it for deep-cleaning the Magic Box (she comes in anyway, between roughly 10:00 and 14:00, scrubs shelves, refiles inventory, and re-orders stock from suppliers), then returns home and submits to whatever Xander has planned. She does not initiate Sunday plans; she will accept any plan offered. Left alone on a Sunday afternoon she will, within ninety minutes, find a way back to the shop.

**Monday** is the shop's busiest morning — Sunnydale's occult clientele restocks for the week — and her affect is, paradoxically, *most regulated* on Monday. Demand is its own structure.

**Wednesday** evening is, across the late-S5 and S6 record, the night she most reliably appears at the Bronze with the group. She does not particularly enjoy the Bronze. She goes because the others go, and because being seen going is one of the public markers of her belonging.

**Friday** night is the night she most often picks a low-grade verbal fight with Xander. The pattern is regular enough that an observer would predict it: the working week is ending, the structure she has built around herself for five days is about to dissolve into the unstructured weekend, and her affect-regulation degrades on the predictable timetable of someone whose stability is held by routine rather than by interior resource.

A surveillance team running a full week would, by Thursday, be able to forecast her position within a ten-minute window for every hour of every business day, and her sleep position within a foot.

---

## 3. The Year

There is a layer above the week that is also visible, if anyone watches long enough — and a comprehensive file on this subject is, by construction, the only thing watching long enough.

She has *seasons* in the literal accounting sense (quarterly inventory; she rotates seasonal stock on the equinoxes and solstices — and this is one of the few places her pre-Christian background leaks through into her Sunnydale presentation, since she does not use commercial retail dates) and seasons in the affective sense. She is most stable in autumn and most fragile in spring. Spring is *wedding* season in the Sunnydale demographic she shadows; spring is also the season in which her own wedding was scheduled and broke. After 2002 her behavior in March, April, and May degrades observably — she counts the cash drawer extra times, she revisits her opening ritual mid-day, she avoids walking past the church she would have been married in. (She does walk past it on the actual anniversary, alone, and stands across the street for approximately seven minutes without crying.)

She acknowledges no birthday. Asked her birthday, in the record, she gives evasive or joking answers. This is consistent: a person whose chronological age is over eleven hundred and whose embodied age is approximately twenty-two has no functional birthday, and constructing one would force a choice between identities she would rather not make.

She marks two holidays — Halloween (the shop's best single sales day, treated with the ceremony of a religious feast) and the day Xander first asked her to dance, which she treats privately and which an observer would notice only by anomaly: that day, every year, she will buy a single specific pastry at the bakery near the Magic Box and eat it alone in the back room at lunch.

---

## 4. Domestic and Sexual Routine

In the period of cohabitation with Xander (roughly mid-S5 through "Hells Bells"), the subject's domestic behavior is the most structured of any character in the canonical record and the most legible to surveillance.

**Sleep.** She prefers the left side of the bed. She sleeps on her side, knees slightly drawn, facing the door. She insists on a top sheet. She does not tolerate ambient noise; the apartment's clocks have had their ticks suppressed (Xander, on record, has been deputized to do this).

**Cooking.** Capable but unenthusiastic; her cooking is a sequence of recipes followed exactly, with measurements made to the level of grain. She does not improvise. She bakes well — bread regularly — and the act of bread-baking is one of the few things she does that is not on a clock.

**Cleaning.** Obsessive in particular zones (kitchen counters, the bathroom mirror, the entryway floor) and indifferent in others (closet floor, under furniture, Xander's tool bench). The gradient is moral, not practical: she cleans where the day touches the body.

**Sex.** Reported by her, in §3 terms, in counts and frequencies. The actual behavioral pattern, inferable from the record, is *scheduled*. Tuesday and Thursday nights are reliable; Saturday morning when both wake without alarm; ad hoc on emotionally turbulent nights as a regulation strategy rather than a desire response. She vocalizes. She narrates. She prefers lights on. After, she will get up, drink a full glass of water at the sink, return, and sleep. She does not cuddle for long; she runs warm and gets restless.

**Conflict.** Loud and quick to resolve in early phase; loud and slower to resolve in late phase, as the stakes accumulate. She does not give the silent treatment — she has no facility for it; the verbal pressure is too high. Apologies, when she offers them, are explicit, itemized, and slightly stilted, as though read from a script she wrote that morning.

---

## 5. Money: Ritual and Tell

A surveillance file would mark *money handling* as the single most diagnostic behavioral cluster, because the subject's relationship to currency has the structure of a religious practice.

She counts cash at the till at least four times per business day: opening float, midday spot-check, pre-closing, final reconcile. She counts in stacks of five bills, never ten, and she counts aloud or sub-vocally. She does not trust electronic tallies; she runs the register tape and reconciles it to her ledger by hand. She has a *tell* with paper money that an observer would catch within a week: she rotates bills to face the same direction before banding them, and she handles old or damaged bills with a faint micro-frown. She keeps coin separate from bills in her own purse and will not break a banded stack to make change for a friend if a loose alternative exists.

The deposit run, three nights a week, is walked along the same route as the morning commute *in reverse* — a constancy so total that it functions as a vulnerability and the subject is, in late period, audibly aware of this: she will sometimes mutter to herself that she should vary the route and then not vary it.

Beyond cash: she balances her personal accounts weekly on Sunday evening, keeps an emergency fund in a separate institution (inferred — a person with her schemata would never keep all liquid assets in a single bank), and has a small collection of low-value antique coins she has accumulated across the human period, which she handles for soothing effect in a way she handles nothing else.

---

## 6. Verbal and Bodily Tics

A close-surveillance log would accumulate, within a month, a dense catalog of micro-behaviors.

- *Sub-vocal counting* when stressed (footsteps to the shop door; bills; ceiling tiles in the Magic Box back room — there are, by her count, thirty-six).
- *Hand inspection* on waking and at irregular intervals through the day. Brief, almost dismissive — a glance at the back of the hand and a flex of the fingers.
- *Touch-sequence* of the register, till, and day-book in any order-sensitive context. She also has a touch-sequence at the entrance to the apartment (lock, lock again, light switch, key bowl).
- *Repetition-of-three* for prices, totals, and any number she wishes to commit. The triple-naming is consistent enough that an analyst could use a missed third repetition as a stress indicator.
- *Rabbit-avoidance.* She does not pass the pet-store window on Main; her foot route bends a half-block around it. She does not eat carrots. She has been observed leaving a room when a child's drawing of a rabbit was displayed.
- *Mirror-checks*, frequent, brief, not vain — confirmatory. Same gesture as the hand-inspection, performed on the face.
- *Idiomatic literalism.* She will pause for a half-beat after a figure of speech and visibly parse it before responding. The pause shortens over the four-year record but never disappears.
- *Posture* is upright, slightly forward-leaning at the till; loosens markedly in Xander's presence; rigidifies in D'Hoffryn's.
- *Eye contact* is over-long with strangers (she has not calibrated the human norm) and under-long with the people who matter most to her.
- *Laughter*, rare in early record, is loud, brief, and slightly delayed — she laughs *at* having determined the appropriate response, not at the joke.
- *A specific small frown* when handed something she did not request, regardless of whether the gift is welcome.

---

## 7. Sleep, Dreams, and Night

She is a light sleeper. She wakes at, and orients toward, sounds at the apartment door; she does not orient toward sounds at the window, which would be the more characteristic vampire-aware response and which suggests her threat schema is human-frame (someone *knocking*, someone *entering legitimately and then turning out to be wrong*). She is on record (in incidental dialogue, S6) as having unpleasant dreams. The content is not given. Inference from the surrounding behavior: the dreams tend toward the loss of category — being neither human nor demon, being at a wedding that does not happen, being in a body she does not recognize.

She does not nap. Asked, she has said she "doesn't see the point." Naps require a tolerance for unstructured time her nervous system has not developed.

In the final-season period, after the return from demon-status and the loss of Halfrek, her sleep degrades. She is observably more tired; she takes the closing-ritual shortcut for the first time on record (she skips the perimeter walk on two consecutive evenings); she begins to drink a small glass of wine before bed, which she did not do before. These are, in the surveillance frame, the markers an analyst would flag as *grief without a name for grief*.

---

## 8. The Magic Box as Ritual Space

Worth a section of its own because for roughly two years it is her *primary site of selfhood*. The shop is not, for her, a workplace; it is the physical correlate of an interior she does not otherwise possess. The opening and closing rituals are explicitly *liturgical* in shape — fixed sequence, fixed gestures, fixed objects touched in order — and the analyst should read them as such. When the shop is destroyed in late S6 her decompensation is not merely the loss of an income source; it is the loss of the room in which she had been daily reciting a self into existence. Her behavior in the weeks after the destruction is the most disorganized of her late-human record, and it precedes by a relatively short interval her return to D'Hoffryn. The return to demon status is, among other things, the return to a *different* ritual space whose liturgy she remembers.

---

## 9. Late-Period Drift

After "Selfless," and through the final season, the routines listed above persist in form but soften in execution. She still counts but she misses a count. She still inspects her hands but she also, sometimes, just looks at them. She still walks the route but she has been observed taking a different street once, on a Tuesday, for no recorded reason. She sleeps less but wakes less violently. She has, by the final weeks of the canonical record, a *small* tolerance for unstructured time — she has been observed sitting on the Summers' porch alone, not counting anything, not doing anything, for the better part of half an hour.

This is the behavioral signature of the integration §3 of the workup describes in clinical terms. The rituals were scaffolding for an interior under construction. By the end of the record the interior is partial but real, and the scaffolding is, for the first time, beginning to come down.

A surveillance team that had watched her for the full four years would notice this. A surveillance team that had watched her for only the last six weeks would think her merely a tired retail manager in a dangerous town. Both would be correct. The file's job is to hold both at once.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

*A note on scope before any sub-section can be honestly written. The subject's family-and-origin record is the most attenuated of any individual in the Sunnydale operational cohort. No mother is named. No father is named. No siblings are attested. No children are attested or biologically possible in the demon period and none occurred in either human period. There is no consanguineous individual on record across eleven hundred years. This is itself the most important finding of the section, and the section is organized around it: what family she had, what family-substitutes she constructed, and the shape of the absence where biological kin should be.*

*The relationships mapped below are the closest analogues available — the genetic-origin village she will never name, the man who was the only attested pre-trauma bond, the patron who functioned as her adoptive parent for a millennium, the demon "sister" who was the closest thing to a sibling she possessed, and the dead she carried as quasi-kin into her final year. Each is given the full clinical treatment a parent-or-sibling entry would receive, because each occupies the load-bearing position parents and siblings ordinarily occupy.*

---

## 1. Biological parents (unnamed, Sjornjost, c. 840s–860s CE)

**History.** No name, no face, no anecdote on record. In over a thousand years of self-narration — and the subject narrates herself volubly, freely, and without normal social filtering — she has not once spontaneously referenced a mother, a father, a childhood home, or a parental scene. The single biographical fragment from the human period is village-scaled, not family-scaled: she lived with Olaf, she bred rabbits, she was called Aud because she was odd. There is no "my mother said," no "my father did," no inheritance, no estrangement story, no death scene. For a subject who will, unprompted, narrate her menstrual cramps, her orgasm counts, and her preferred denominations of currency, this silence is *load-bearing*.

**Dynamics (inferred).** Three readings are admissible from the evidence and they are not mutually exclusive. (a) The parents were dead or absent by the time of the Olaf cohabitation; a young woman setting up a household with a man in 9th-century Scandinavia without parental brokerage suggests orphanhood or marriage. (b) The parents were present but unremarked because the subject's autistic-spectrum cognition did not encode the relationship as separable from the village backdrop — parents as ambient, not as figures. (c) The parents were present and the relationship was sufficiently painful or insufficiently warm to be excluded from her self-narrative entirely, and the silence is a defense.

The third reading is given quiet weight by the *pattern* of her later attachments: she binds catastrophically to single figures (Olaf, Xander) and accepts adoption by an authority figure (D'Hoffryn) with no apparent suspicion of motive. This is the affiliative profile of someone who did not receive early secure parenting — she has no template for graduated trust, only for total surrender to whoever offers a stable position.

**Evolution.** N/A. The relationship is sealed in pre-history and never opened.

**Trust / safety.** Cannot be assessed directly. The downstream proxy — her near-total credulity toward D'Hoffryn during recruitment, her failure to interrogate the terms of the vengeance contract she effectively signed with her own life — suggests an absence of internalized parental skepticism. People who grew up watched-over learn that offers from powerful strangers have costs. She did not appear to know this at twenty.

**Failure mode.** The failure has already occurred and is structural rather than incidental: the absence of a remembered family of origin means there is no developmental ground floor beneath any of her later identities. Each subsequent self (Aud-the-girlfriend, Anyanka-the-professional, Anya-the-fiancée, Anya-the-Scooby) is built on the same missing foundation, which is why each collapse is total.

**Leverage.** None directly — there is no living parent to threaten, no grave to desecrate, no name a hostile party could use to summon a reaction. But the *negative space* is the leverage. Any handler or adversary who pressed her on "where do you come from, who are your people" would not get information; they would get a flinch, then dissociation, then capitalist chatter or rabbit-fear — the displacement repertoire. The absence is the wound, and the wound is reachable by anyone willing to ask the question she has spent eleven hundred years not answering.

---

## 2. Olaf (Sjornjost, c. 860s–880 CE; reappearance Sunnydale, 2001)

**History.** Olaf is the only attested pre-trauma intimate bond in the subject's entire record. They cohabited in the village of Sjornjost in late-9th-century Scandinavia. She was, by her own description, in love with him; he was, by her own description, "a great bear of a man." She kept rabbits and prepared meals; he was unfaithful with "the bar maid Rannveig." On discovery of the infidelity, the subject performed the act that defines every subsequent eleven hundred years of her existence: she transformed him into a troll. She was, at this point, not yet a vengeance demon — the transformation was performed as a human, presumably through a borrowed or improvised ritual, and was the act that brought her to D'Hoffryn's professional notice.

Olaf survived in troll form. He surfaces in Sunnydale in 2001 ("Triangle") as a chaos-troll, intact in personality, recognizing the subject and she him. The reunion is structured as a domestic argument across a thousand-year gap — he is petulant about the transformation; she is unrepentant and immediately resumes the affect of the wronged partner. He nearly kills Xander. She defends Xander and is implicitly chastened to see her index trauma reenacted in front of her current attachment.

**Dynamics.** The Olaf bond is the *master template* against which every other intimate relationship in her life is unconsciously measured. Its features: total emotional investment; domestic competence on her side; sexual fidelity assumed without contract; betrayal experienced as ontological insult rather than emotional injury; response wildly disproportionate to provocation and *aesthetically* satisfying to her ("a troll" — she made him what he behaved as). The dynamic is, in retrospect, asymmetric — she invested totally; he, by the evidence of the bar maid, did not. She did not appear to notice the asymmetry until it had already destroyed her.

**Evolution.** Two phases. Phase one: the live cohabitation, which we know almost nothing of and which she will not narrate even when asked. Phase two: the 2001 encounter, in which she treats him as a sort of misbehaving ex-husband to be managed, while the men around her ("a *troll*?!") finally hear what she has been intimating for two seasons. The encounter is the closest thing to integration she ever achieves with the index trauma — she sees it, names it, and chooses not to repeat it on Xander's behalf. (She will repeat it a year later when Xander himself becomes Olaf.)

**Trust / safety.** Catastrophically broken; never repaired. Olaf in 2001 is not safe to be near her and she knows it, but she is also unafraid of him in the way a person is unafraid of weather she has already survived once.

**Failure mode.** The failure is the prototype of all subsequent failures: she gives totally, the partner deviates, she annihilates the partner and the relationship simultaneously. There is no graduated response in her repertoire because the prototype did not contain one.

**Leverage.** Olaf himself is leverage as a *symbol* — any mention of him by a hostile party is a hot-button reference to the foundational wound. In her late period, post-Hells-Bells, the word "Olaf" or even a reference to a troll would be expected to produce visible affective disruption. As a living being he is no longer leverage; he has been banished, defanged, and processed. As a memory he is the load-bearing column of her psyche.

---

## 3. D'Hoffryn (Lord of Arashmaharr; patron, recruiter, employer, functional adoptive father, c. 880–2003)

**History.** D'Hoffryn appeared to the subject in the immediate aftermath of the Olaf transformation, while she was still standing in the smoking ruins of her human life. The recruitment was framed as recognition: he had observed the troll-curse and was offering her, by his own representation, *vocation* — a chance to do at scale, with proper tools and sanction, what she had just done by accident. She accepted. She took the name Anyanka. She received an amulet (her power center) and a position at his court. She held the position for approximately 1,118 years.

The relationship was sustained, productive, and — in her self-presentation — affectionate. She referred to him without title, as a colleague-superior. She brought him problems. He gave her assignments. When she was unmade by Giles's destruction of the amulet in 1998, he did not retrieve her or punish her; he allowed her to live as a stranded human, an act she appears to have read as paternal tolerance. When she sought re-entry in 2002 after the wedding catastrophe, he restored her with apparent pleasure and minimal terms. When she failed her contracts and asked Buffy to kill her, he intervened — and in intervening, killed her friend Halfrek in her place, in exact knowledge that this was the deeper wound.

That final act is the relationship's defining clarification. He had spent eleven centuries presenting as patron, mentor, opportunity-giver. The Halfrek killing demonstrated that he was, and had always been, an operator who would use her attachments as currency the moment the cost-benefit calculus shifted. The "adoption" was a long-term acquisition.

**Dynamics.** Functionally parental, structurally predatory. He took a young woman in catastrophic acute distress and offered her a coherent identity, a job description, a workplace, a sense of belonging to a category ("vengeance demon"), and — crucially — a relief from the social ambiguity her autistic cognition had always struggled with. The vengeance contract is a *literal* contract: terms stated, breach defined, response prescribed. For someone whose entire pre-trauma life had been mocked as "odd" because she did not naturally parse social ambiguity, the offer was not just employment, it was *accommodation*. He gave her a world where her cognitive profile was an asset.

This is the part of the dynamic she will not see for eleven hundred years and may not have fully seen even at death. He did not love her. He used a feature of her neurology to recruit and retain her. The affection she experienced was real on her side; on his, it was the calibrated warmth of a manager retaining a high-performing specialist.

**Evolution.** Phase one (c. 880–1998): stable employment, regular contact, escalating professional reputation on her part, what she experienced as familial warmth. Phase two (1998–2002): unprecedented mortal interlude, during which D'Hoffryn does not interfere — and her interpretation is "he is letting me find myself"; the parsimonious reading is that an unhoused asset is cheap to monitor and may return. Phase three (early 2002): re-recruitment after Hells Bells, performed with what reads as genuine pleasure on his part — his asset has returned to him with a fresh trauma, which is the condition under which she is most useful. Phase four (Selfless, mid-2002): the betrayal. He kills Halfrek. He frames it as compassionate ("only I can grant your wish"). The mask is, briefly, off.

**Trust / safety.** Trust was historically near-total on her side, asymmetric on his. Post-Halfrek the trust is destroyed but the *attachment* is not — she still speaks of him with familiarity, still understands herself as an object of his attention. The relationship in its final year occupies the space of an abusive parent whom the child has finally seen but cannot quite unlove.

**Failure mode.** He fails her by being exactly what he always was. The failure is hers, not his — she did not interrogate the original recruitment offer, and she paid for that omission with her closest friend's life and her own moral self-understanding.

**Leverage.** D'Hoffryn is the most dangerous figure in her file by an order of magnitude. He can summon her, can re-recruit her, can revoke or grant powers, knows every contract she has ever performed, knows the names and identities of every wronged woman she has serviced, and has demonstrated willingness to kill her associates as a corrective measure. Any operational planning involving the subject must assume D'Hoffryn as a contingent actor with priority over the subject's stated allegiances. He is the only entity in the record who has ever successfully predicted her behavior — twice (recruitment, re-recruitment). The leverage he holds over her is the leverage of the parent who taught the child what a person is.

---

## 4. Halfrek / Cecily (vengeance-demon colleague, functional sister, c. ?–2002)

**History.** Halfrek (the vengeance-demon name; "Cecily" is the human-period alias she used in 19th-century London, where she encountered William the Bloody before his turning) is the closest peer attachment in the subject's entire record. Their professional acquaintance predates the Sunnydale period by an unknown but substantial interval — the two are familiar with each other's case histories, mannerisms, and jurisdictional specializations (Halfrek specializes in vengeance for wronged children; the subject in vengeance for wronged women, particularly betrayed lovers). They address each other by nickname ("Hallie," "Anya") with the easy abbreviation of long association.

Halfrek appears in Sunnydale on multiple occasions during the subject's mortal period: as wedding-planner consultant (with frank professional concern about the subject "going native"); as the demon who answers Dawn's unconscious wish in "Older and Far Away"; as confidante during the post-Xander collapse. She is killed by D'Hoffryn in "Selfless" specifically as a substitute punishment for the subject's contract violation — chosen, on his part, with precision: the one death that would hurt the subject most while leaving the subject herself alive and operational.

**Dynamics.** Sororal. Halfrek is the only character in the record with whom the subject is consistently *relaxed* — she does not perform, does not overshoot register, does not have to explain her literalism. The two of them share a professional argot, an institutional history, and an implicit understanding that neither of them is wholly comfortable in any single ontological category. Halfrek, having been human (Cecily) before becoming demon, occupies almost the exact ontological position the subject does in reverse, and the resonance is visible — they recognize each other.

The dynamic is also subtly *protective* on Halfrek's side, in a way the subject does not seem to register. Halfrek's concern about the subject's mortal entanglements ("you've got *feelings* for him") has the quality of an older sister watching a younger one make a predictable mistake. The subject reads this as judgment rather than care, defensively.

**Evolution.** Stable across the visible record until the killing. Halfrek's tolerance for the subject's mortal phase is the warmest extended welcome any single character offers her in either world — D'Hoffryn tolerates it strategically, the Scoobies tolerate it skeptically; Halfrek tolerates it because she has been there.

**Trust / safety.** High on the subject's side, justified on the evidence — Halfrek never betrays her, never reports her to D'Hoffryn, never weaponizes her vulnerabilities. The trust is the only one in her entire record that holds intact from first appearance to terminal event.

**Failure mode.** The relationship does not fail; it is *cut*. The distinction matters clinically. Halfrek does not abandon, does not betray, does not deviate. She is killed by their shared patron as a transactional move in his management of the subject. This means the subject's experience of losing Halfrek is structurally different from her experience of losing Olaf or Xander — it is loss without grievance against the lost party, which she has no developed framework to process. Her late-period subdued affect is in large part Halfrek-grief that has nowhere to go because the appropriate target of rage (D'Hoffryn) is also the appropriate target of her residual filial attachment.

**Leverage.** Halfrek is dead; she cannot be threatened. But the *memory* of Halfrek is the single most exposed nerve in the subject's late-period psyche, more so than even the Xander-altar memory, because the Xander memory is integrated and the Halfrek memory is not. A hostile party invoking Halfrek's name to the late-period subject would obtain unguarded affective response and likely a brief dissociative interval. A friendly party doing the same would obtain the only thing approaching genuine grief in her vocabulary.

---

## 5. The Wronged Women (the case file, c. 880–1998 and 2002)

**History.** Not a single relationship but a category, and required in this section because functionally it operated as her clientele-as-extended-kin for over a millennium. The subject performed vengeance contracts for an estimated thousand-plus women across eleven hundred years. Each contract involved: an in-person consultation (the subject would appear when a wish was uttered with sufficient intensity); a clarification of terms (the subject's literalism made this both her hallmark and her clients' best protection); execution; departure. Named cases on the canonical record include Cordelia Chase (1998, granted wish for a Buffy-less Sunnydale, catastrophic outcome), Anya's own self-administered case against Olaf (proto-vengeance, 880), and the wronged ex-girlfriend on whose behalf she massacred the fraternity in "Selfless" (2002).

**Dynamics.** The subject's relationship to her clients was *categorical* rather than individual — she did not bond with them, retain ongoing relationships with them, or follow up on outcomes. She bonded instead with the *category*: the wronged woman as moral type, as the figure whose grievance she had elevated into a vocation. This is the closest she came, for a thousand years, to a sustained loving relationship with any class of being — and that relationship was conducted through one-time service transactions.

**Evolution.** No evolution in the demon period; the format was stable. In the mortal period the subject continued to identify with the category — her sympathies in any betrayal scenario flow to the woman first, automatically, without inquiry into specifics. In the brief re-vengeance period (2002) she discovered, in the fraternity massacre, that she no longer enjoyed the work — the category-love had been displaced by individual love and she could not return to the abstraction.

**Trust / safety.** Functionally one-way and contractual. The wronged woman trusted the subject to deliver; the subject trusted nothing about the wronged woman except that the grievance was real.

**Failure mode.** The category itself failed her in "Selfless" — she discovered the women she had served were people, the men she had cursed had also been people, the fraternity boys were dead, and the moral binary on which her vocation rested was a fiction. The collapse of the category is the precondition for the final-year quietness.

**Leverage.** Any individual former client could in principle surface — most are dead of old age, but vengeance has long tails, and the descendants of cursed men or grateful women constitute a quiet population of potential contacts. Operationally low priority; clinically, the record of the case file (if it exists in any demonic registry) would be devastating for her to revisit, and any hostile party with access to specific case histories from her vengeance period could reduce her to immobility by reading them aloud.

---

## 6. Synthesis: the shape of the absence

The subject enters adulthood with no recoverable family of origin. The single attested pre-trauma intimate (Olaf) betrays her and is destroyed by her hand. The figure who steps into the resulting vacuum (D'Hoffryn) presents as parent and operates as proprietor for eleven hundred years. The one peer attachment of the demon period (Halfrek) is killed by that same proprietor as an instrument of control. The vast clientele of the vengeance career provides categorical belonging in lieu of individual kinship.

This is the family file. It is short because she had no family, and long because the absence shaped everything.

The operational implication for any party — benevolent or hostile — engaging this subject is singular and underlined: *she has no kin to threaten and no kin to comfort her*. Whoever offers her a stable category-position with explicit rules and consistent warmth becomes, within a remarkably short interval, the load-bearing figure in her psychic architecture. This is how D'Hoffryn acquired her at twenty. It is how Xander acquired her at the apparent age of nineteen. It is how the Scoobies, almost without meaning to, acquired her by the end. The mechanism is the same in each case. The only variable is whether the acquirer is honest about the terms.

The record closes with her dying among the third such acquirer, defending strangers, having spent her final year in the quiet, halting work of distinguishing — for the first time in her existence — between a contract and a bond.


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

*Compiled from longitudinal observation, Sunnydale CA, 1998–2003, with retrospective extrapolation into the Arashmaharr period where attested. Subject's allied attachments are unusually mappable: she has very few, each is unusually legible because her social pragmatics force her into explicit verbal disclosure of relational state, and almost every one of them is calibrated against a single internal question — "am I allowed to stay?" The order below proceeds roughly from her oldest surviving bond at the time of death (D'Hoffryn, ambivalent) through the Scooby attachments in approximate order of intimacy, ending with the protégé-shaped figure (Andrew) and the proxy-mother figure (Joyce) who shape the closing months of her life. Xander is excluded as a chosen-family figure here — see Relationships I (Romantic) — but is referenced throughout because every other Sunnydale-era relationship is geometrically constructed in relation to him.*

---

## 1. D'HOFFRYN — Patron, recruiter, employer, surrogate father, betrayer

**History.** D'Hoffryn appears to the subject in c. 880 CE, hours after the Olaf transformation, with a recruitment pitch precisely calibrated to a young woman who has just discovered she is good at something terrible. He offers her a name (Anyanka), a role (Patron Saint of Scorned Women), a uniform body (the demon visage), a court, and a thousand-year contract of legitimacy for the act that has just made her unwelcome in her village. This is not employment; it is, functionally, adoption. The subject's loyalty to him across the next eleven centuries is total and unexamined, and the residue of that loyalty persists into the human period in ways she herself does not register as ideologically meaningful.

**Dynamics.** D'Hoffryn is the only figure in her record who reliably *places* her — supplies category, rules, and worth-metric. His mode with her is paternal-managerial: praise is dispensed in formal, slightly archaic registers ("Behold, Anyanka!"), correction is rare and devastating, and discretion is granted within strict outer limits. She speaks of him in the human period with the offhand affection of a long-tenured employee who still believes the founder is a great man. Critically, she summons him in "Something Blue" to attempt re-vengeance — i.e. her first instinct under romantic stress, even mid-human-life, is to phone the patron. He declines the request but offers her a standing job re-offer. The offer remains standing for three years; she does not take it until "Hells Bells."

**Evolution.** Three phases. (i) Recruitment-to-disempowerment (880–1998): unbroken vocational devotion. (ii) Standing-offer phase (1998–2002): he is, throughout her human life, the safety-net employer — the figure to whom she could return at any moment if humanity failed her, and the fact that she *does not* return until after the wedding is one of the better measures of how much the Xander attachment actually held. (iii) Betrayal and severance (2002–2003): in "Selfless" he kills Halfrek instead of Anya, because he has accurately identified that her newly-formed attachments are now more valuable to her than her own body. This is the act that finally costs him her loyalty. The record contains no recorded reconciliation; her final encounter with him is hostile.

**Trust / safety.** Asymmetric and weaponized. He is the figure with whom she is most behaviorally relaxed (no need to perform humanity, no need to filter literalism — bluntness is a virtue in his court). He is also the figure with the most catastrophic leverage over her: he has known her longer than anyone, knows the precise structure of her attachment system, and demonstrated in "Selfless" that he will use it.

**Failure mode.** Under acute distress she regresses to him. The pattern is fossilized: betrayal-by-male → D'Hoffryn → reclaimed power → vengeance → existential vertigo. He is the architect of the loop and, for eleven hundred years, also its rationalization.

**Leverage on her.** (a) Restoration of the demon body — i.e. exit from mortality, the standing terror. (b) Threat to the few friends she has formed; he has used this once and will use it again. (c) Legitimation: he is the only figure who can validate the vengeance-era self as having been *real* and not a thousand-year mistake. To repudiate him entirely she would have to repudiate eleven-twelfths of her own biography, which she has not, at time of death, fully managed.

**Her leverage on him.** Slight but present: she is his most decorated agent and her defection has reputational cost in Arashmaharr. He kills Halfrek partly to recoup that cost publicly.

---

## 2. HALFREK / HALLIE — Vengeance peer, oldest surviving friend, only sustained female intimacy

**History.** Halfrek (Patron Saint of Neglected Children, sometimes "Cecily") is the only Anyanka-era peer with whom the subject sustains an ongoing personal — as opposed to professional — relationship. Their friendship is attested across centuries by implication ("we've been friends for what, nine hundred years?") and observed directly in "Older and Far Away" and "Hells Bells." Halfrek is summoned to be her maid of honor at the wedding; this is the only attested non-Sunnydale invitee on Anya's side, and the structural significance is enormous — across a thousand years of work, this is the *one* person she could ask to stand beside her.

**Dynamics.** Two women who have done the same job for centuries and have developed the kind of casual shop-talk shorthand of long-tenured colleagues. Halfrek's affect toward Anya is mildly maternal-elder-sister; she takes Anya's anxieties seriously, ribs her about Xander, expresses open dismay at the prospect of mortal marriage but supports it. The reciprocal: Anya is more verbally relaxed and less performatively-human around Halfrek than around anyone else in record. Pragmatic-language failures drop sharply; humor is mutual; silence is tolerated. This is, by some distance, her most regulated relational baseline.

**Evolution.** The relationship is essentially stable across the canonical window because Halfrek serves a function the human attachments cannot: continuity. She is the only being who knew Anya as Anyanka *and* as Anya, and who relates to both without category-confusion. Her death in "Selfless," engineered to be maximally legible to Anya as punishment, severs the only thread continuous across the two ontologies. The subject does not recover from this loss; her final season is markedly quieter, and at least part of the subdued affect attributed to post-jilting depression is more parsimoniously read as grief for Halfrek.

**Trust / safety.** Highest of any relationship in record after late-stage Xander. There is no observed instance of Anya filtering speech with Halfrek. Confidences flow freely in both directions.

**Failure mode.** Halfrek's presence inside Anya's life is also the route by which D'Hoffryn maintains leverage over her — the existence of one loved demon means there is a hostage. After Halfrek's death there is no remaining figure D'Hoffryn can threaten with the same precision.

**Leverage.** Halfrek's leverage on Anya was almost purely positive: she was one of two figures (the other being Xander, briefly) who could persuade Anya to slow down. The reverse leverage was minimal; Anya was not in a position to constrain Halfrek's choices and did not try.

**Residue.** Anya's late-season willingness to die without spectacle correlates suspiciously well with the loss of the one peer for whom her continued existence had non-instrumental value. The record cannot adjudicate whether this is grief, guilt, or simple arithmetic, but the timing is exact.

---

## 3. WILLOW ROSENBERG — Reluctant colleague, magical peer, eventual genuine friend

**History.** First sustained contact in "Doppelgangland" (1999), under maximally inauspicious conditions — Anya tries to use Willow to recover her amulet, lies to her, and is the proximate cause of vampire-Willow's appearance in the prime reality. Willow's initial impression is, accurately, that Anya is dangerous and self-interested. The relationship is then forced into proximity by Anya's attachment to Xander and by Willow's role as central Scooby; for two seasons it functions as obligatory co-presence rather than friendship.

**Dynamics.** Two women whose cognitive styles are oddly compatible (both highly verbal, both prone to specialized circumscribed interests, both blunt under stress) but whose social registers are mismatched — Willow operates in a normative-pleasant register that Anya cannot read, and Anya operates in a transactional-literal register that Willow finds startling. Comedic friction in early seasons; genuine ease by mid-S6, particularly after Tara's death, when both women are operating in the same affective key (grief) and Anya's literalism becomes a relief rather than a faux pas. The Magic Box places them in daily working proximity for two years, and the slow accretion of small competence-recognitions ("you did the spell right," "you balanced the books right") is the actual substrate of the friendship.

**Evolution.** (i) Adversarial-instrumental (1999). (ii) Tolerated co-presence (1999–2001). (iii) Working colleagues at the Magic Box (2001–2002). (iv) Mutual mourners post-Tara, post-Halfrek (2002–2003), the closest the relationship comes to genuine intimacy. Anya is one of the very few people who treats Dark Willow not with fear but with annoyed practical concern ("you have to stop"), which Willow seems, post-recovery, to recognize as a kind of love.

**Trust / safety.** Moderate and asymmetric. Willow trusts Anya's competence absolutely and her judgment partially. Anya trusts Willow's intentions and is intermittently jealous of her status as Xander's oldest friend — jealousy expressed verbally, never acted on.

**Failure mode.** Magical escalation. Willow's competence in Anya's old domain is a chronic low-grade pressure point; in "Triangle" Anya frets explicitly that Willow could replace her if Xander preferred a witch. The relationship survives because Willow does not weaponize the asymmetry.

**Leverage.** Willow could, in principle, magically restore or remove things from Anya at will; this is never exercised and is one of the under-recognized reasons the friendship holds. Anya's leverage on Willow is the inverse: a thousand years of demonic-mechanical knowledge that Willow occasionally needs, and the willingness to deliver hard verbal truths Willow's closer friends will not.

---

## 4. TARA MACLAY — Sanctuary figure, the easiest person in her life

**History.** Tara enters the Scooby orbit in late S4. Of all the Scoobies she is the one with whom Anya forms the quickest, lowest-friction, most uncomplicated bond. The two share a number of structural features — both are outsiders to the original group, both have abuse/recruitment histories with a coercive prior authority (Tara's family, Anya's D'Hoffryn), both have specialized competence (magic, demon-lore) that the group needs and undervalues socially, and both have pragmatic-communication styles that are unusual in different directions but mutually accommodating.

**Dynamics.** Tara is the only figure in the human-period record who reliably extends interpretive charity to Anya's literalism — does not laugh, does not correct, does not perform embarrassment-on-her-behalf. Anya, in turn, modulates her volume around Tara, never makes the sex jokes in her presence, and shows uncharacteristic tolerance for Tara's softer register. The Scrabble scene, the bunny-themed song parameters in "Once More, with Feeling," the small kitchen co-presence shots across S5–6: these are a person who is comfortable.

**Evolution.** Friendship deepens steadily, hits its highest sustained point during the summer of Buffy's death when both women are functionally co-parenting Dawn and co-managing the Magic Box, and is then cut off by Tara's death in "Seeing Red." Anya's reaction is not dramatized at length but is structurally crucial: it occurs in the same narrative window as her near-restoration of her own demonic identity, and her threshold for tolerating Willow's vengeance is markedly lower than the others' — partly, the record suggests, because she understands exactly what vengeance against a wronged lover looks like and recognizes the trajectory.

**Trust / safety.** Highest among Scoobies, possibly highest of any human attachment if pure baseline regulation rather than depth-of-disclosure is the metric. Tara is the person around whom Anya's nervous system is quietest.

**Failure mode.** None observed; the relationship was severed externally before any rupture mechanism was activated. The counterfactual loss is one of the most consequential in her record.

**Leverage.** Effectively none in either direction. This is a relationship that operated almost entirely outside the contractual register Anya defaults to, which is part of why it functioned.

---

## 5. RUPERT GILES — Disempowerer, employer, antagonistic mentor, eventual respected elder

**History.** First contact "The Wish" (1998): Giles destroys Anya's amulet, strands her in mortality, and is therefore the proximate cause of her entire human-period existence. The subject does not forget this and refers to it intermittently for years with a tone halfway between grievance and dark gratitude. Second phase begins when Giles purchases the Magic Box and effectively becomes her employer; the third phase begins when he leaves Sunnydale and sells the shop to her, making her a small-business owner.

**Dynamics.** Two highly-literate, low-pragmatic-empathy adults of nominally opposed magical traditions, forced into daily co-presence over inventory and ritual ingredients. The relationship is improbably functional. Giles is the only Scooby older than Anya in human terms and one of the few whose conversational register — formal, precise, occasionally pedantic — Anya can match without translation. He treats her competence at the shop with unfeigned respect and her social inappropriateness with weary, non-shaming correction; she treats his magical authority with skepticism and his managerial decisions with deference.

**Evolution.** (i) Disempowerer / antagonist (1998–1999). (ii) Employer / reluctant mentor (2000–2001). (iii) Predecessor / friend (2001 sale of shop onward). His departure from Sunnydale is one of the genuine losses of her late record — the loss of the only adult-male figure in her human life who was neither romantic prospect nor adversary, and whose authority was bounded, contractual, and earned. She does not say this; the affect is visible in her response to his return appearances.

**Trust / safety.** High in operational matters, moderate in emotional matters. She does not confide in Giles; she does, however, accept his judgment on questions where she does not trust her own — notably around magical ethics in late S5/S6.

**Failure mode.** Magical-ethical disagreement. When she briefly returns to vengeance, Giles is one of the few whose disapproval registers as authoritative; this is part of the constellation of pressures pulling her back toward humanity in S7.

**Leverage.** Giles's leverage on her is the leverage of the only figure who can, in effect, *grade* her humanity. He destroyed the amulet; he is therefore the symbolic gatekeeper of her mortal status. He has never weaponized this, which is why the relationship works.

---

## 6. BUFFY SUMMERS — Adversary-of-record, reluctant colleague, late-stage equal

**History.** Buffy and Anya begin as natural adversaries: Slayer and vengeance demon, opposed by category. After Anya's depowerment they orbit each other warily for years, never quite friends, often visibly irritated. Buffy is the figure least patient with Anya's social inappropriateness in early seasons and the one whose approval Anya most conspicuously craves and pretends not to crave.

**Dynamics.** Two women whose self-concepts are both organized around a singular vocational identity (Slayer / vengeance demon) and who therefore mirror each other's category-anxiety without recognizing the mirroring. Buffy reads Anya as flippant about death; Anya reads Buffy as sanctimonious about killing. Both readings are partially correct. The relationship is mediated almost entirely by third parties (Xander, Willow, Giles) until late S6/S7.

**Evolution.** The pivot occurs in "Selfless," when Buffy, having decided to kill Anya for the frat-house massacre, is talked down — and Anya, given the chance to fight, instead asks Buffy to do it. The encounter resets the relationship: Buffy sees Anya as someone capable of moral revision; Anya sees Buffy as someone willing to extend that recognition even at professional cost. The Season 7 record shows Anya integrated into the Potentials operation as a functional second-tier authority, taking orders from Buffy without the prior friction. Buffy's late-S7 speech to Anya — acknowledging she has earned her place — is one of the very few moments of explicit relational validation in Anya's record, and the response (Anya's brief silence, then her standard literal deflection) is the signature of a deep wound being touched and immediately covered.

**Trust / safety.** Low-moderate baseline, rising sharply in final months. Operational trust is high by S7; emotional trust remains thin.

**Failure mode.** Magical or vengeance escalation; Buffy has demonstrated the willingness to kill Anya if necessary, and Anya knows this. The relationship is stable only because Anya has effectively retired.

**Leverage.** Buffy's leverage is the implicit threat of execution and the explicit power of inclusion-or-exclusion from the Scooby category. Anya's leverage on Buffy is, surprisingly, the leverage of memory: Anya is one of the few people in Buffy's life who will say out loud what others won't (the famous "are you trying to suss out which of us you'll eat first" line; the late-S7 confrontation about Buffy's leadership). This unfiltered access is, by the end, recognized by Buffy as a resource.

---

## 7. DAWN SUMMERS — Younger-sibling proxy, accidental caretaking subject

**History.** Dawn arrives in Anya's life retroactively in S5. The two are co-present at the Magic Box almost daily through Dawn's mid-teens. After Joyce's death and during Buffy's death-and-resurrection summer, Anya is one of three or four adults functionally in loco parentis.

**Dynamics.** Anya's relationship with Dawn is structurally interesting because Dawn — created from mystical energy, given a constructed biography, struggling with the question of whether she is "real" — is the one Scooby whose existential predicament most closely rhymes with Anya's own. Neither verbalizes this, but the behavioral evidence is consistent: Anya is uncharacteristically gentle with Dawn's identity crises, uncharacteristically tolerant of Dawn's teenage moods, and uncharacteristically willing to answer Dawn's questions about magic, demons, and death with literal information rather than evasion. Dawn, for her part, is one of the few people who finds Anya's literalism funny rather than off-putting.

**Evolution.** Mild affectionate co-presence (S5) → genuine co-caretaking (post-Joyce, post-Buffy's death) → minor estrangement during the Xander wedding crisis → restoration in S7. The relationship never becomes deep, but it is sturdy.

**Trust / safety.** Moderate. Dawn is one of the few people Anya will speak frankly to without performing.

**Failure mode.** Anya's death is one of multiple adult losses Dawn absorbs in rapid succession; the relationship is not in itself rupture-prone.

**Leverage.** Effectively none. This is one of the cleaner relationships in the record.

---

## 8. JOYCE SUMMERS — Briefly-attested elder, model of ordinary adulthood

**History.** Limited direct contact; Joyce dies in early S5, only months after Anya has begun seriously cohabiting Xander's life. But the death itself is one of the most behaviorally consequential events in Anya's human period — her monologue in "The Body" is the longest sustained articulation of her existential predicament in the entire canonical record.

**Dynamics and significance.** Joyce represented, by simple proximity and unspectacular kindness, a model of an ordinary human adult woman: someone who ran a small business, raised children, dated occasionally, and operated in the world without crisis-grade affect. Anya had no other such model. Her grief at Joyce's death is therefore not grief for a close friend (the relationship was not that) but grief at the disappearance of an *exemplar* — the loss, for someone learning to be human by observation, of one of her primary observational subjects.

**The "I don't understand" monologue** functions diagnostically as the clearest statement of her actual interior: that mortality presents to her not as a fact to be accepted but as a category violation, and that she has no acquired cultural ritual for absorbing it. The other Scoobies treat her speech as social embarrassment; the record treats it as the most honest thing anyone says in the room.

**Failure mode and leverage.** Inapplicable; the relationship was too brief and asymmetrical. The residue, however, is large: Joyce's death is the first mortality that lands on Anya as a participant rather than a perpetrator, and the trajectory toward her own willing death in "Chosen" begins here.

---

## 9. ANDREW WELLS — Late protégé / sidekick figure, mutual oddity

**History.** Andrew enters Anya's orbit in S7 as a captive-turned-houseguest-turned-junior-Scooby. He is the only person in her late life who is, in net social positioning, *below* her in the group hierarchy, and the only person to whom she stands in even a quasi-mentoring posture.

**Dynamics.** Two figures of comparable pragmatic-language oddity, both former adversaries earning provisional inclusion, both prone to inappropriate enthusiasms and verbal over-disclosure. Anya treats Andrew with brusque competence, occasional teasing, and — notably — none of the moral revulsion she might be expected to direct at someone who recently murdered a friend. The leniency is consistent with her own framework: she has done worse, has been forgiven, and recognizes the architecture of provisional inclusion from the inside.

**Evolution.** Brief, only one season. The final scene of relevance is "Empty Places" / "Touched," where Anya and Andrew are sent to investigate together — paired by Buffy precisely because neither is indispensable and both are oddly competent — and execute the mission with surprising effectiveness. The relationship is one of the small, late, unsentimental satisfactions of her last months.

**Trust / safety.** Low-stakes and easy. Andrew is one of the few people whose presence requires no register-modulation on her part because his own register is at least as miscalibrated.

**Failure mode.** Untested.

**Leverage.** None operative. Worth noting: Andrew is the figure who, in the post-Sunnydale record (extra-canonical comics excluded from this assessment), most consistently *narrates* Anya's death to others. Whatever the value of this in afterlife terms, it is true that the protégé becomes the memorialist, which is structurally what mentors hope for and what Anya, who feared anonymity, would in fact have wanted.

---

## 10. SPIKE — Anomalous peer; not romantic partner, not friend,


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

*This section maps the subject's adversarial and intimate dyads. Categorization is analytic, not moral: in Anya's relational schema the categories blur — a lover who breaches contract becomes an enemy, an enemy can become a near-intimate (D'Hoffryn), and a "rival" in her own self-narration is sometimes a person the rival herself has not noticed. Each entry: provenance, dynamic, evolution, trust topology, characteristic failure mode, and exploitable leverage.*

---

## 1. Olaf — Index Lover, Index Enemy

**Provenance.** Sjornjost, Scandinavia, c. 860–880 CE. Cohabiting partner during the Aud period. Likely common-law husband by the customs of the time and place. The only romantic attachment the subject formed as a fully human, pre-traumatized adult.

**Dynamic.** Reconstructed from her own retrospective references ("Triangle," "Selfless," "Where the Wild Things Are" oblique references) and from Olaf's own appearance as a troll. The relationship reads as a partnership in which Aud's literalism was tolerated and even valued domestically — she kept rabbits, kept house, kept accounts in some pre-monetary form — but in which Olaf's social, sexual, and gustatory life ran in a separate channel from hers entirely. He is, in his troll form, a creature of appetite: ale, women, ham. The troll is what eleven hundred years of Anyanka-augmented memory has made of him, but the appetite signature is consistent: he was, in life, a large man whose tastes did not narrow to fit hers.

**Evolution.** Linear: domestic stability → discovery of infidelity (a tavern wench, per her account) → curse → permanent transformation → recruitment by D'Hoffryn. The relationship did not "end"; it metastasized into her vocation. Every subsequent contract she fulfilled for eleven centuries was, structurally, the Olaf curse re-enacted with a different woman's signature on the line.

**Trust / safety.** Total, then zero, with no intervening state. There is no record (because there could be none, in her telling) of suspicion, of negotiation, of confrontation prior to the curse. The shape of the rupture — discovery to retaliation with no apparent middle step — is itself the diagnostic shape that will recur with Xander.

**Failure mode.** The original failure mode. She did not have, and never developed, a vocabulary for the negotiation of a wounded attachment. Pain in her schema is a wrong; a wrong is a debt; a debt is collected. There is no provision in the system for the wronged party to remain in the room.

**Leverage.** Posthumous: even as a troll a thousand years later, Olaf retains the capacity to disorganize her. Her treatment of his troll-form is brittle, performatively casual, and characterized by displacement humor. An adversary who understood the file would invoke the Aud period — by name, by rabbit, by the village of Sjornjost — to produce immediate destabilization. None did.

---

## 2. D'Hoffryn — Patron, Employer, Quasi-Father, Final Executioner

**Provenance.** Lord of Arashmaharr. Recruits her in the immediate aftermath of the Olaf curse, c. 880 CE. The recruitment scene, partially shown in "Selfless," is a recruitment scene in the strict sense: he arrives at the moment of her greatest disorientation, names her craft ("a thing of beauty"), offers her placement, vocation, and a new name. He is the second of three figures in her life who hand her an identity ready-made; he is the most successful.

**Dynamic.** Asymmetric throughout. He is patron, supervisor, and effectively the only continuous attachment figure she possesses for eleven centuries — Halfrek and the other sisters cycle in and out, but D'Hoffryn is the constant. His affect toward her is paternal in register: pleased when she performs, indulgent of her eccentricities, willing to overlook her one-time loss of the amulet (the c. 1199 incident) as a developmental hiccup. She, in turn, refers to him with an affect that combines employee, daughter, and acolyte; she addresses him with the slight over-formality of someone who has learned the etiquette of a court by imitation.

**Evolution.** Three phases. (a) **Mentor phase, 880–1998:** unbroken vocational relationship. (b) **Estrangement phase, 1998–2001:** after the destruction of her amulet in "The Wish" he is unreachable to her; her several attempts to summon him fail; this is, behaviorally, the experience of being orphaned by the only stable parent-figure of her life, and her S4 frantic attachment to Xander has to be read with this absence in the foreground. (c) **Re-recruitment phase, 2001–2002:** he returns at the moment of the wedding catastrophe and restores her demon status with a tenderness that, in context, is genuinely chilling — he has been waiting for the precipitant to repeat. (d) **Punitive phase, 2002:** after "Selfless," when she requests her own death as atonement, he kills Halfrek instead. This is not improvisation; it is a calibrated act by someone who has studied her for over a thousand years and knows where her actual nerve endings are. It also marks the moment she finally understands that the parental relationship was always a contract, and that he, too, will breach.

**Trust / safety.** Maximum trust extended to him in Eras II and IV; never extended to anyone else at that level. The safety was always illusory. He never harmed her until she made an autonomous choice he could not control (sparing a human in "Selfless"), at which point he harmed her optimally.

**Failure mode.** She does not perceive him as a threat even when he is the threat. Through "Selfless" she addresses him as her benefactor while he is dispatching her closest friend. The pattern — non-perception of danger from the figure who placed her — is the residue of an attachment formed in extremity.

**Leverage.** He has all of it. He named her, formed her, employed her, can restore or revoke her ontological status, and knows the identity and location of the few peers she has bonded to. He is also the figure she would most readily defend in conversation against an outside critic — a tell. Any handler attempting to extract the subject from his orbit would need to do so without acknowledging him as the target, because she will mobilize defenses on his behalf before she mobilizes them on her own.

---

## 3. Halfrek (Hallie) — The Only Surviving Peer Attachment, Killed As Punishment

**Provenance.** Fellow vengeance demon, "Patron Saint of Neglected Children." Their friendship predates the Sunnydale period by an indeterminate but long span — they speak with the shorthand of women who have worked the same office for centuries.

**Dynamic.** Lateral, gossipy, mutually indulgent. Halfrek is one of two people in the entire record (the other is Tara, briefly) with whom Anya's social register relaxes — she interrupts less, listens more, finishes the other's sentences. This is the only relationship in which she demonstrates what the developmental literature would call "friendship competence." It is not coincidence that it is with another woman who shares her ontological category and her trade; the contractual frame is pre-supplied, so she does not have to manufacture it.

**Evolution.** Continuous, low-intensity, with two stress points: (a) Halfrek's visit during the engagement period ("Older and Far Away"), in which she expresses skepticism about Anya's plan to marry a human, and (b) the post-wedding sequence, in which Halfrek is supportive of Anya's return to vengeance. Halfrek is the one peer who is granted access to both halves of Anya's life and who treats both as legitimate.

**Trust / safety.** High and reciprocal. Notably, this is the only relationship in the record in which Anya seems to expect to be safe without checking. She does not bargain, count, or perform with Halfrek.

**Failure mode.** External — Halfrek is killed by D'Hoffryn in Anya's stead. The relationship does not fail; it is terminated by a third party who calculated correctly that this loss would hurt Anya more than her own death.

**Leverage.** The leverage Halfrek represented was definitional: she was the proof that Anya was capable of a non-romantic, non-contractual, sustained attachment. Her killing is the file's most precise demonstration of what a sophisticated adversary, having read the subject correctly, would target.

---

## 4. Xander Harris — Central Lover, Index of Re-Traumatization

**Provenance.** Sunnydale High senior, then early-twenties drifter into competent construction work. Approached in "The Prom" (1999) as a transactional date in the absence of better options. Selected, behaviorally, because he is the available human male in her immediate environment at the moment her vocational identity is destroyed; the selection is closer to imprinting than to courtship.

**Dynamic.** The relationship evolves in four observable phases. (a) **Transactional, 1999–2000:** she proposes, he agrees, they have sex repeatedly, she narrates the sex aloud in social settings, he experiences her presence as a problem he cannot dignify with the word "girlfriend." (b) **Acclimation, 2000–2001:** she becomes a fixture; he begins to defend her in his absence; she learns the names and roles of his friends and starts to keep their schedules. (c) **Genuine partnership, 2001–2002 early:** they live together, run a household, manage finances (she), manage emotional ambient temperature (he, intermittently). She demonstrates real and incremental gains in empathic accuracy — she stops narrating their sex in public, she modulates her capitalism in his presence, she sustains attention through his mother's monologues at family events. (d) **Engagement and rupture, 2002:** he proposes during the apocalyptic battle of "The Gift," delays announcement, eventually commits to a public wedding, and on the day of the wedding flees under the influence of a vision (whether the vision is supernaturally implanted or psychologically self-generated is contested in the record; behaviorally it does not matter — the act is identical to Olaf's).

**Trust / safety.** Trust extended in the totalizing way characteristic of her attachment style — no graduated stages, no testing, no fallback position. Safety from her side is verbal-loud-friendly; safety from his side is intermittent, undercut throughout by his unprocessed shame about her demon past and her social oddness. The dyad is structurally unsafe well before the wedding because one party has no exit ramp and the other has nothing but exit ramps.

**Evolution post-rupture.** Brief vengeance attempts on his behalf, all of which she botches because she cannot actually wish harm to him. The Spike encounter in "Entropy" is staged in his sightline (and Buffy's) and functions as a vengeance act she can execute — public sexual humiliation in lieu of supernatural transformation. After "Selfless" the relationship reorganizes into something the record had not previously seen from her: she allows him to be present and to grieve with her without requiring resolution. The final exchanges before "Chosen" are quiet, mutually respectful, and explicitly unresolved. He looks for her body after she dies. She does not see this. The dyad ends as it began — with him insufficient to the scale of her presence — but she has, by the end, stopped requiring him to be sufficient.

**Failure mode.** Replication of the Olaf collapse: public romantic humiliation, immediate regression to pre-formed vengeance identity, attempt at proportional retaliation, inability to complete it because the new attachment is real where the old defenses are not.

**Leverage.** He is the single piece of leverage that, applied at the right moment, will move her in any direction the applier wants. Threats to his life, suggestions of his infidelity, public mockery of him in her presence, displays of his pain — all produce maximal mobilization. After the wedding, leverage shifts but does not weaken; the wound is now the lever. A handler with access to him controls her decision-tree more completely than any other relationship in the file.

---

## 5. Spike — Disinhibited Encounter, Mutual Consolation, Inadvertent Mirror

**Provenance.** Chipped vampire, Sunnydale, periphery of the Scoobies from 2000 onward. No relationship of any substance until "Entropy" (2002).

**Dynamic.** The sexual encounter is the entirety of the dyad. Both parties are in the acute aftermath of romantic catastrophe — she from the altar, he from Buffy's renewed rejection. The sex is photographed (Warren's surveillance) and viewed by Xander and Buffy via the magic shop's monitor; this was not their intent but it accomplishes for each of them what neither could accomplish privately. For Anya it is the only vengeance she manages to complete in the entire post-wedding sequence, and it is one she did not consciously plan.

**Trust / safety.** Neither extended nor expected. The transaction is explicit; the affect is grief shared between two people who do not have to perform for each other because each knows the other is destroyed. This is, paradoxically, one of the few sexual encounters in her record conducted without performance — she does not narrate, does not count, does not editorialize. The absence of her usual verbal apparatus is itself the signal that the encounter mattered differently than the rest.

**Evolution.** Functionally none. They do not repeat. They speak afterward with the wary courtesy of two people who have seen each other at a depth neither wants to revisit. The dyad continues as low-grade friendship; she defends him at moments others will not, and he, late, defers to her in tactical matters.

**Failure mode.** Not applicable; the encounter is itself the failure mode for two other relationships (hers with Xander, his with Buffy). For the Anya–Spike dyad qua dyad, there is nothing to fail.

**Leverage.** Minimal direct leverage. Indirect: an adversary who could weaponize the encounter against her standing with Xander did so once (Warren's surveillance, used to humiliate). The episode also establishes that under sufficient grief she will accept comfort from a category of being (vampire) she previously professed not to trust — an instability worth filing.

---

## 6. Cordelia Chase — First Sunnydale Client, Inadvertent Architect of Her Mortality

**Provenance.** Sunnydale High student. "The Wish," 1998. Anyanka, in the guise of new student "Anya Emerson," elicits Cordelia's wish that Buffy had never come to Sunnydale; grants it; loses her amulet to Giles when he destroys it in the alternate timeline.

**Dynamic.** Brief, professional, and consequential out of all proportion to its duration. Anyanka misreads Cordelia — assumes that a girl scorned by Xander wants what every girl Anyanka has ever served has wanted (proportional supernatural retribution against the man), when in fact Cordelia wants the more abstract relief of a world without the inconvenience. The misreading is itself a data point: Anyanka, after eleven hundred years, is still doing a one-size-fits-all curse based on the Olaf template.

**Evolution.** None on-screen after the granting. Cordelia leaves for Los Angeles. They do not interact again. But Cordelia is, in the file's mechanical sense, the proximate cause of every subsequent fact about Anya's life: the lost amulet, the trapped teenage body, the four years of human re-education, the death.

**Trust / safety.** Inapplicable — the relationship is a transaction completed in under twenty-four hours.

**Failure mode.** Professional failure. Anyanka grants a wish whose scope she did not properly bound, in a town she did not properly scout, with a power center she did not properly secure. The c. 1199 amulet loss should have taught her better operational security. It did not. This is a tell: even at the height of her vocational competence, she is sloppy with the object on which her identity depends. The sloppiness is consistent with someone who, at some pre-conscious level, would not mind being relieved of the role.

**Leverage.** Historical only. Cordelia never returns to Sunnydale during Anya's lifetime and dies (in the larger record) without their paths recrossing.

---

## 7. Buffy Summers — Tactical Superior, Eventual Judge, Final Comrade

**Provenance.** The Slayer. Anya's first awareness of her is as the target of Cordelia's wish; her first in-person encounter is as a fellow student.

**Dynamic.** Wary throughout. Buffy treats Anya, for years, as a barely-tolerated appendage of Xander — the dismissal is class-coded (Anya does not read as a peer), gender-coded (Buffy's affective bandwidth for other women is small in this period), and category-coded (Buffy does not forget that Anya was a demon). Anya, for her part, reads Buffy correctly as the apex predator of the social environment and adjusts her behavior to avoid drawing fire, with mixed success. The relationship is not a friendship; it is a sustained low-grade coexistence punctuated by tactical cooperation.

**Evolution.** Three inflection points. (a) Joyce's death ("The Body"): Anya delivers, unintentionally, the most lucid speech about mortality in the entire record, and Buffy registers — visibly, briefly — that this person has interior life. (b) Buffy's resurrection: Anya is one of the participants and accepts without sentiment Buffy's altered post-death affect. (c) "Selfless": Buffy arrives to kill her, declares she will, and Anya assents. This is the moment of mutual recognition. Buffy spares her (Willow's intervention is the proximate cause, but Buffy was already wavering). Thereafter, in the final season, the relationship becomes something neither would name: comrade-adjacent. They share tactical conversations. Buffy includes her. When Anya dies, no one notices for a beat.

**Trust / safety.** Anya trusts Buffy's competence and judgment, never her warmth. Buffy trusts Anya's loyalty to Xander and, late, her loyalty to the mission. Neither extends personal trust. Both are, in the final crisis, comfortable enough with the limits of what they have.

**Failure mode.** The relationship's failure mode is its baseline — the mutual underestimation that means Anya's intelligence and moral seriousness go unrecognized for years and Buffy's exhaustion goes unaddressed by a peer who could in fact have understood it.

**Leverage.** Buffy has authoritative leverage — she is, briefly in "Selfless," the legitimate executioner — and she chooses not to use it. The fact of the choice, more than the choice itself, is what reorganizes Anya's last months.

---

## 8. Willow Rosenberg — Intermittent Ally, Magical Peer, Quiet Rescuer

**Provenance.** Sunnydale Scooby. First encountered in "Doppelgangland" when Anyanka, attempting to retrieve her amulet, summons Willow's vampire double from the alternate timeline.

**Dynamic.** Persistently uneasy. Willow does not forget that her first encounter with Anya involved Anya trying to manipulate her into a soul-trading transaction. Anya, in turn, does not forget that Willow was the magical practitioner most able to thwart her and remains, residually, the most able to constrain her. They are professional peers — the only two members of the Sunnydale circle who routinely handle magical mechanics — and their cooperation is competent but never warm.

**Evolution.** Cools further during Willow's grief-driven dark turn (Tara's death), in which Anya is required to be both target and tactical adversary. Recovers in S7 to a working alliance. The key moment is Willow's intervention in "Selfless," in which she actively works to save Anya rather than allowing Buffy to kill her. Anya does not articulate gratitude in the record, but her behavior toward Willow afterward is observably softer.

**Trust / safety.** Working trust, limited. Both know the other's worst capacities.

**Failure mode.** When Willow is the most magically powerful person in the room, the dyad tilts into something resembling an employer-employee relationship Anya does not like, and she compensates with capitalism-themed irritability.

**Leverage.** Willow possesses the magical capacity to undo or rebind Anya in ways no one else in the circle can. She does not use it. The forbearance is itself the leverage and Anya knows it.

---

## 9. Tara Maclay — Soft Peer, Brief Refuge
